text-align:center;">Topics</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center"><div class="hlist">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/List_of_conflicts_in_Ireland" style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">\n<a href="/wiki/File:Four_Provinces_Flag.svg" class="image"><img alt="Four Provinces Flag.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Four_Provinces_Flag.svg/16px-Four_Provinces_Flag.svg.png" width="16" height="8" class="noviewer" title="Portal:Ireland">Ireland&#32;portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_Ireland" title="Template:History of Ireland"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_Ireland" title="Template talk:History of Ireland"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</b> was established by the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1800" title="Acts of Union 1800">Acts of Union 1800</a>, which merged the kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Great Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Ireland</a>. \n</p><p>The United Kingdom having financed the European coalition that defeated France during the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, developed a large <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> that led the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> to become the foremost <a href="/wiki/World_power" class="mw-redirect" title="World power">world power</a> for the next century. The <a href="/wiki/Eastern_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern War">Crimean War</a> with Russia and the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Boer wars</a> were relatively small operations in a largely peaceful century.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Rapid industrialisation that began in the decades prior to the state\'s formation continued up until the mid-19th century. The <a href="/wiki/Great_Irish_Famine" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Irish Famine">Great Irish Famine</a>, exacerbated by government inaction in the mid-19th century, led to <a href="/wiki/Demographic_collapse" class="mw-redirect" title="Demographic collapse">demographic collapse</a> in much of Ireland and increased calls for Irish land reform.\n</p><p>The 19th century was an era of rapid economic modernization and growth of industry, trade, and finance, in which Britain largely dominated the world economy. Outward migration was heavy to the principal British overseas possessions and to the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. The Empire was expanded into most parts of Africa and much of South Asia. The Colonial Office and India Office ruled through a small number of administrators who managed the units of the Empire locally, while democratic institutions began to develop. <a href="/wiki/British_India" class="mw-redirect" title="British India">British India</a>, by far the most important overseas possession, saw a short-lived revolt in 1857. In overseas policy, the central policy was <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a>, which enabled British and Irish financiers and merchants to operate successfully in many otherwise independent countries, as in South America.  London <a href="/wiki/Splendid_isolation" title="Splendid isolation">formed no permanent military alliances</a> until the early 20th century, when it began to cooperate with <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_Third_Republic" title="French Third Republic">France</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a>, and moved closer to the United States.\n</p><p>Growing desire for <a href="/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule_movement" title="Irish Home Rule movement">Irish self-governance</a> led to the <a href="/wiki/Irish_War_of_Independence" title="Irish War of Independence">Irish War of Independence</a>, which resulted in most of Ireland seceding from the Union and forming the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a> in 1922. <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> remained part of the Union, and the state was renamed to the current "United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland" in 1927. The modern-day United Kingdom is the same country as the one from this period\xe2\x80\x94a direct continuation of what remained after the secession\xe2\x80\x94not an entirely new <a href="/wiki/Successor_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Successor state">successor state</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#1801_to_1820"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">1801 to 1820</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Union_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Union of Great Britain and Ireland</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Napoleonic_Wars"><span class="tocnumber">1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Napoleonic Wars</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Postwar_reaction:_1815\xe2\x80\x931822"><span class="tocnumber">1.4</span> <span class="toctext">Postwar reaction: 1815\xe2\x80\x931822</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Age_of_Reform:_1820\xe2\x80\x931837"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Age of Reform: 1820\xe2\x80\x931837</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Protestant_Nonconformists"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Protestant Nonconformists</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 process</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-15"><a href="#Chartism"><span class="tocnumber">2.3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Chartism</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Leadership"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Leadership</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-17"><a href="#Prime_Minister_Wellington"><span class="tocnumber">2.4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Prime Minister Wellington</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-18"><a (1811\xe2\x80\x931889)</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Victorian_era"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span policy</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-25"><a href="#Free_trade_imperialism"><span class="tocnumber">3.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Free trade imperialism</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-26"><a href="#Russia,_France_and_the_Ottoman_Empire"><span class="tocnumber">3.1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Russia, France and the Ottoman Empire</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-27"><a href="#American_Civil_War"><span class="tocnumber">3.1.3</span> <span class="toctext">American Civil War</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-28"><a href="#Empire_expands"><span class="tocnumber">3.1.4</span> <span class="toctext">Empire expands</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Leadership_2"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Leadership</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30"><a href="#Queen_Victoria"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Queen Victoria</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-31"><a href="#Disraeli"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Disraeli</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-32"><a href="#Gladstone"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Gladstone</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-33"><a href="#Salisbury"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Salisbury</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-34"><a href="#Morality"><span class="tocnumber">4.5</span> <span class="toctext">Morality</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#Early_20th_century"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Early 20th century</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-36"><a href="#Edwardian_era:_1901\xe2\x80\x931914"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Edwardian era: 1901\xe2\x80\x931914</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-37"><a href="#Great_War"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Great War</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-38"><a href="#Postwar"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Postwar</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-39"><a href="#Ireland"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Ireland</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-40"><a href="#Campaign_for_Irish_Home_Rule"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Campaign for Irish Home Rule</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-41"><a href="#Irish_Rebellion"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Irish Rebellion</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#List_of_monarchs"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">List of monarchs</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-43"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-44"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-45"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-46"><a href="#Historiography"><span class="tocnumber">10.1</span> <span class="toctext">Historiography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-47"><a href="#Primary_sources"><span class="tocnumber">10.2</span> <span class="toctext">Primary sources</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-48"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1801_to_1820">1801 to 1820</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: 1801 to 1820">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Georgian_era" title="Georgian era">Georgian era</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Regency" class="mw-redirect" title="British Regency">British Regency</a>, <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Georgian_society" class="mw-redirect" title="Georgian society">Georgian society</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Union_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland">Union of Great Britain and Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Union of Great Britain and Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A brief period of limited independence for Ireland came to an end following the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Rebellion_of_1798" title="Irish Rebellion of 1798">Irish Rebellion of 1798</a>, which occurred during the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars" title="French Revolutionary Wars">British war with revolutionary France</a>. The British government\'s fear of an independent Ireland siding against them with the French resulted in the decision to unite the two countries. This was brought about by <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1800" title="Acts of Union 1800">legislation in the parliaments of both kingdoms</a> and came into effect on 1 January 1801. The Irish had been led to believe by the British that their loss of legislative independence would be compensated with <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Emancipation" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic Emancipation">Catholic Emancipation</a>, that is, by the removal of <a href="/wiki/Civil_disabilities_placed_upon_Roman_Catholics" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil disabilities placed upon Roman Catholics">civil disabilities placed upon Roman Catholics</a> in both Great Britain and Ireland. However, <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">King George III</a> was bitterly opposed to any such Emancipation and succeeded in defeating his government\'s attempts to introduce it.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Napoleonic_Wars">Napoleonic Wars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Napoleonic Wars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Army_during_the_Napoleonic_Wars" title="British Army during the Napoleonic Wars">British Army during the Napoleonic Wars</a></div>\n<p>During the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Second_Coalition" title="War of the Second Coalition">War of the Second Coalition</a> (1799\xe2\x80\x931801), Britain occupied most of the French and Dutch overseas possessions, the Netherlands having become a satellite state of France in 1796, but tropical diseases claimed the lives of over 40,000 troops. When the Treaty of Amiens ended the war, Britain agreed to return most of the territories it had seized. The peace settlement was in effect only a ceasefire, and Napoleon continued to provoke the British by attempting a trade embargo on the country and by occupying the city of <a href="/wiki/Hanover" title="Hanover">Hanover</a>, capital of the <a href="/wiki/Electorate_of_Brunswick-L%C3%BCneburg" title="Electorate of Brunswick-L\xc3\xbcneburg">Electorate</a>, a German-speaking duchy which was in a <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a> with the United Kingdom. In May 1803, war was declared again. Napoleon\'s plans to invade Great Britain failed, chiefly due to the inferiority of his navy, and in 1805 a <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> fleet led by <a href="/wiki/Horatio_Nelson,_1st_Viscount_Nelson" title="Horatio Nelson, 1st Viscount Nelson">Nelson</a> decisively defeated the French and Spanish at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Trafalgar" title="Battle of Trafalgar">Trafalgar</a>, which was the last significant naval action of the Napoleonic Wars.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1806, Napoleon issued the series of <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Decree" title="Berlin Decree">Berlin Decrees</a>, which brought into effect the <a href="/wiki/Continental_System" title="Continental System">Continental System</a>. This policy aimed to eliminate the threat from the British by closing French-controlled territory to foreign trade. The <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> remained a minimal threat to France; it maintained a standing strength of just 220,000 men at the height of the Napoleonic Wars, whereas France\'s armies exceeded a million men\xe2\x80\x94in addition to the armies of numerous allies and several hundred thousand <a href="/wiki/National_Guard_(France)" title="National Guard (France)">national guardsmen</a> that Napoleon could draft into the French armies when they were needed. Although the Royal Navy effectively disrupted France\'s extra-continental trade\xe2\x80\x94both by seizing and threatening French shipping and by seizing French colonial possessions\xe2\x80\x94it could do nothing about France\'s trade with  the major continental economies and posed little threat to French territory in Europe. France\'s population and agricultural capacity far outstripped that of the British Isles, but it was smaller in terms of industry, finance, mercantile marine and naval strength.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Napoleon expected that cutting Britain off from the European mainland would end its economic hegemony. On the contrary Britain possessed the greatest industrial capacity in the world, and its mastery of the seas allowed it to build up considerable economic strength through trade to its possessions and the United States.  The Spanish uprising in 1808 at last permitted Britain to gain a foothold on the Continent. The Duke of Wellington gradually pushed the French out of Spain, and in early 1814, as Napoleon was being driven back in the east by the Prussians, Austrians, and Russians, Wellington invaded southern France. After Napoleon\'s surrender and exile to the island of Elba, peace appeared to have returned. Napoleon suddenly reappeared in 1815. The Allies united and the armies of Wellington and Blucher defeated Napoleon once and for all at Waterloo.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War_of_1812_with_the_United_States">War of 1812 with the United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: War of 1812 with the United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Signing_of_Treaty_of_Ghent_(1812).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Signing_of_Treaty_of_Ghent_%281812%29.jpg/220px-Signing_of_Treaty_of_Ghent_%281812%29.jpg" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Signing_of_Treaty_of_Ghent_%281812%29.jpg/330px-Signing_of_Treaty_of_Ghent_%281812%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Signing_of_Treaty_of_Ghent_%281812%29.jpg/440px-Signing_of_Treaty_of_Ghent_%281812%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1400" data-file-height="967" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Signing_of_Treaty_of_Ghent_(1812).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1814 signing of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Ghent" title="Treaty of Ghent">Treaty of Ghent</a> ending the war with the United States; by A. Forestier <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1915</span></div></div></div>\n<p>To defeat France, Britain put heavy pressure on the Americans, seizing merchant ships suspected of trading with France, and impressing sailors born in Britain, regardless of their claimed American citizenship. British government agents armed Indian tribes in Canada that were raiding American settlements on the frontier.  The Americans felt humiliated and demanded war to restore their honor, despite their complete unpreparedness. The <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> was a minor sideshow to the British, but the American army performed very poorly, and was unable to successfully attack Canada. In 1813, the Americans took control of Lake  Erie and thereby of western Ontario, knocking most of the Indian tribes out of the war.  When Napoleon surrendered for the first time in 1814, three separate forces were sent to attack the Americans in upstate New York, along the Maryland coast (burning Washington but getting repulsed at Baltimore), and up the Mississippi River to a massive defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_New_Orleans" title="Battle of New Orleans">Battle of New Orleans</a>.  Each operation proved a failure with the British commanding generals killed or in disgrace. The war was a stalemate without purpose. A negotiated peace was reached at the end of 1814 that restored the prewar boundaries.  British Canada celebrated its deliverance from American rule, Americans celebrated victory in a "second war of independence,"  and Britain celebrated its defeat of Napoleon.  The treaty opened up two centuries of peace and open borders.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Postwar_reaction:_1815.E2.80.931822"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Postwar_reaction:_1815\xe2\x80\x931822">Postwar reaction: 1815\xe2\x80\x931822</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Postwar reaction: 1815\xe2\x80\x931822">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Britain emerged from the Napoleonic Wars a very different country than it had been in 1793. As industrialisation progressed, society changed, becoming more urban. The postwar period saw an economic slump, and poor harvests and inflation caused widespread social unrest. British leadership was intensely conservative, ever watchful of signs of revolutionary activity of the sort that had so deeply affected France. Historians have found very few signs, noting that social movements such as Methodism strongly encouraged conservative support for the political and social status quo.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>The major constitutional changes included a reform of Parliament, and a sharp decline in the power and prestige of the monarchy.  The Prince regent, on becoming King <a href="/wiki/George_IV" class="mw-redirect" title="George IV">George IV</a> in 1820 asked Parliament to divorce his wife Queen <a href="/wiki/Caroline_of_Brunswick" title="Caroline of Brunswick">Caroline of Brunswick</a> so that he could marry his favorite lover. Public and elite opinion strongly favoured the Queen and ridiculed the king.  The fiasco helped ruin the prestige of the monarchy and it recovered a fraction of the power wielded by King George III in his saner days. Historian Eugene Black says:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>the damage was irrevocable.  The sovereign was increasingly a symbolic contradiction in his own age.  Through madness, stupidity, and immorality Victoria\'s three predecessors lowered the stock of monarchy.  Only thirty years of the narrow domestic virtues of Queen Victoria finely retrieved the symbolic luster of the sovereign.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ultra_Tories">Ultra Tories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Ultra Tories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ultra-Tories" title="Ultra-Tories">Ultra-Tories</a></div>\n<p>The Ultra-Tories were the leaders of reaction and seemed to dominate the Tory Party, which controlled the government.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>  Every untoward event seemed to point to a conspiracy on the left which necessitated more repression to head off another terror such as happened in the  <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> in 1793.  Historians find that the violent radical element was small and weak; there were a handful of small conspiracies involving men with few followers and careless security; they were quickly suppressed.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>  Nevertheless, techniques of repression included the <a href="/wiki/Habeas_Corpus_Act_1816" title="Habeas Corpus Act 1816">suspension of Habeas Corpus in 1817</a> (allowing the government to arrest and hold suspects without cause or trial). <a href="/wiki/Henry_Addington,_1st_Viscount_Sidmouth" title="Henry Addington, 1st Viscount Sidmouth">Sidmouth</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Gagging_Acts" title="Gagging Acts">Gagging Acts</a> of 1817 heavily muzzled the opposition newspapers; the reformers switched to pamphlets and sold 50,000 a week.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Peterloo_Massacre_and_the_Six_Acts">Peterloo Massacre and the Six Acts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Peterloo Massacre and the Six Acts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p>In industrial districts in 1819, factory workers demanded better wages, and demonstrated. The most important event was the <a href="/wiki/Peterloo_Massacre" title="Peterloo Massacre">Peterloo Massacre</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a>, on 16 August 1819, when a local militia unit composed of landowners charged into an orderly crowd of 60,000 which had gathered to demand the reform of parliamentary representation. The crowd panicked and eleven died and hundreds were injured.  The government saw the event as an opening battle against revolutionaries. In reaction Liverpool\'s government passed the "<a href="/wiki/Six_Acts" title="Six Acts">Six Acts</a>" in 1819. They prohibited drills and military exercises; facilitated warrants for the search for weapons; outlawed public meetings of more than 50 people, including meetings to organize petitions; put heavy penalties on blasphemous and seditious publications; imposing a fourpenny stamp act on many pamphlets to cut down the flow on news and criticism. Offenders could be harshly punished including exile in Australia. In practice the laws were designed to deter troublemakers and reassure conservatives; they were not often used.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Norman_Gash" title="Norman Gash">Norman Gash</a> says "Peterloo was a blunder; it was hardly a massacre." It was a serious mistake by local authorities who did not understand what was happening.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, it had a major impact on British opinion at the time and on history ever since as a symbol of officialdom brutally suppressing a peaceful demonstration thinking mistakenly that it was the start of an insurrection.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> By the end of the 1820s, along with a general economic recovery, many of the  repressive laws of the 1810s were repealed  and in 1828 new legislation guaranteed the civil rights of religious dissenters.\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Ultra_Tories:_peak_and_decline">Ultra Tories: peak and decline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ultra Tories: peak and decline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p>The Ultra-Tories peaked in strength about 1819\xe2\x80\x9322 then lost ground inside the Tory Party. They were defeated in important breakthroughs that took place in the late 1820s in terms of tolerating first dissenting Protestants.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> An even more decisive blow was the unexpected repeal of the many restrictions on Catholics, after widespread organized protest by the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Association" title="Catholic Association">Catholic Association</a> in Ireland under <a href="/wiki/Daniel_O%E2%80%99Connell" class="mw-redirect" title="Daniel O\xe2\x80\x99Connell">Daniel O\xe2\x80\x99Connell</a>, with support from Catholics in England.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>  Sir <a href="/wiki/Robert_Peel" title="Robert Peel">Robert Peel</a> was alarmed at the strength of the Catholic Association, warning in 1824, "We cannot tamely sit by  while the danger is hourly increasing, while a power co-ordinate with that of the Government is rising by its side, nay, daily counteracting its views."<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Prime Minister Wellington, Britain\'s most famous war hero, told Peel, "If we cannot get rid of the Catholic Association, we must look to Civil War in Ireland sooner or later."<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>  Peel and Wellington agreed that to stop the momentum of the Catholic  Association it was necessary to pass <a href="/wiki/Catholic_emancipation" title="Catholic emancipation">Catholic emancipation</a>, which gave Catholics the vote and the right to sit in Parliament. That happened in 1829 using Whig support. Passage demonstrated that the veto power long held by the ultra-Tories no longer was operational, and significant reforms were now possible across the board. The stage was set for the Age of Reform.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Age_of_Reform:_1820.E2.80.931837"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Age_of_Reform:_1820\xe2\x80\x931837">Age of Reform: 1820\xe2\x80\x931837</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Age of Reform: 1820\xe2\x80\x931837">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The era of reform came in a time of peace, guaranteed in considerable part by the overwhelming power of the Royal Navy.  Britain engaged in only one serious war between 1815 and 1914, the Crimean war against Russia in the 1850s.  That war was strictly limited in terms of scope and impact.  The major result was the realisation that military medical services needed urgent reform, as advocated by the nursing leader <a href="/wiki/Florence_Nightingale" title="Florence Nightingale">Florence Nightingale</a>.  British diplomats, led by <a href="/wiki/Henry_John_Temple,_3rd_Viscount_Palmerston" title="Henry John Temple, 3rd Viscount Palmerston">Lord Palmerston</a>, promoted British nationalism, opposed reactionary regimes on the continent, helped the Spanish colonies to free themselves, and worked to shut down the international slave trade.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It was a time of prosperity, population growth, and better health, except in Ireland where over one million deaths were caused by a terrible famine when the potato crop failed in the 1840s.  The Industrial Revolution accelerated, with textile mills joined by iron and steel, coal mining, railroads, and shipbuilding.  The second British Empire, founded after the loss of the 13 American colonies in the 1770s, was dramatically expanded in India, other parts of Asia, and Africa.  There was little friction with other colonial powers until the 1890s.  British foreign policy avoided entangling alliances.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>  \n</p><p>Britain from the 1820s to the 1860s experienced a turbulent and exciting \xe2\x80\x9cage of reform\xe2\x80\x9d.  The century started with 15 years of war against France, ending in Wellington\xe2\x80\x99s triumph against Napoleon in 1815 at Waterloo.  There followed 15 difficult years, in which the Tory Party, representing  a small, rich landed aristocracy that was fearful of a popular revolution along the French model, employed severe repression.  In the mid-1820s, however, as popular unrest increased, the government made a series of dramatic changes.  The more liberal among the Tories rejected the ultraconservative \xe2\x80\x9cUltra Tory\xe2\x80\x9d faction.  The party split, key leaders switched sides, the Tories lost power, and the more liberally minded opposition Whigs took over.  The Tory coalition fell apart, and it was reassembled under the banner of the Conservative Party.  Numerous Tories, such as Palmerston, switched over to the Whig opposition, and it became the Liberal Party.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Constitutionally, the 1830s marks a watershed: the end of Crown control over the cabinet.  King William IV in 1834 was obliged to accept a Prime Minister who had a majority in Parliament, and the Crown ever since has gone along with the majority.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The great Reform Act of 1832 came at a time of intense public and elite anxiety and broke the logjam.  The parliamentary system, based on a very small electorate and large numbers of seats that were tightly controlled by a small elite, was radically reformed. For the first time the growing industrial cities had representation in Parliament.  This opened the way for another decade of reform that culminated in the repeal of the Corn Laws in 1846\xe2\x80\x94ending the tariff on imported grain that kept prices high for the landed aristocracy. Repeal was heavily promoted by the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Corn_Law_League" title="Anti-Corn Law League">Anti-Corn Law League</a>, grass roots activists led by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Cobden" title="Richard Cobden">Richard Cobden</a> and based in the industrial cities; they demanded cheap food.  There were a series of reforms of the electoral laws, expanding the number of male voters and reducing the level of corruption.  The reactionary Tory element was closely linked to the Church of England, and expressed its strong hostility toward Catholics and nonconformist Protestants by restricting their political and civil rights.  The Catholic started to organise in Ireland, threatening instability or even civil war, and the moderates in Parliament emancipated them.  The Nonconformists were similarly freed from their restrictions.  In addition to reforms at the Parliamentary level, there was a reorganisation of the governmental system in the rapidly growing cities, putting a premium on modernisation and expertise, and large electorates as opposed to small ruling cliques.   A rapidly growing middle class, as well as active intellectuals, broaden the scope of reform to include humanitarian activities such as a new poor law and factory laws to protect women and children workers.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protestant_Nonconformists">Protestant Nonconformists</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Protestant Nonconformists">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist" title="Nonconformist">Nonconformist</a></div>\n<p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Asa_Briggs" title="Asa Briggs">Asa Briggs</a> finds that in the 1790\xe2\x80\x931815 period there was an improvement in morals.  He identifies the cause as the religious efforts by evangelicals inside the Church of England,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> and Dissenters or Nonconformist Protestants. Briggs sees a genuine improvement in morals and manners as people:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>became wiser, better, more frugal, more honest, more respectable, more virtuous, than they ever were before."  Wickedness still flourished, but the good were getting better, as frivolous habits were discarded for more serious concerns. The leading moralist of the era, <a href="/wiki/William_Wilberforce" title="William Wilberforce">William Wilberforce</a>, saw everywhere "new proofs presenting themselves of the diffusion of religion".<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>Nonconformists, including <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Congregationalists" class="mw-redirect" title="Congregationalists">Congregationalists</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Baptists" title="Baptists">Baptists</a> and the rapidly growing <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">Methodist denomination</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">Quakers</a>, <a href="/wiki/General_Assembly_of_Unitarian_and_Free_Christian_Churches" title="General Assembly of Unitarian and Free Christian Churches">Unitarians</a> and smaller groups.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>  They were all outside the established Church of England (except in Scotland, where the established church was Presbyterian), They proclaimed a devotion to hard work, temperance, frugality, and upward mobility, with which historians today largely agree. A major Unitarian magazine, the <i>Christian Monthly Repository</i> asserted in 1827:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>Throughout England a great part of the more active members of society, who have the most intercourse with the people have the most influence over them, are Protestant Dissenters.  These are manufacturers, merchants and substantial tradesman, or persons who are in the enjoyment of a competency realized by trade, commerce and manufacturers, gentlemen of the professions of law and physic, and agriculturalists, of that class particularly who live upon their own freehold.  The virtues of temperance, frugality, prudence and integrity promoted by religious Nonconformity...assist the temporal prosperity of these descriptions of persons, as they tend also to lift others to the same rank in society.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>The Nonconformists suffered under a series of disabilities, some of which were symbolic and others were painful, and they were all deliberately imposed to weaken the dissenting challenge to Anglican orthodoxy.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>  The Nonconformists allied with the Whigs to demand for civil and religious equality.  Grievances included a 1753 law that to be legally recognized marriage had to take place in the Anglican parish church. The Anglican parish register was the only legally accepted birth documentation. The Anglican parish controlled the only religious.  burial grounds.  Oxford and Cambridge had to reject non-Anglican applicants.  At the local level, everyone who lived in the boundaries of an Anglican church was required to pay taxes to support the parish.  The Test and  Corporation laws required all national and local government officials had to attend Anglican church services.  In February 1828, Whig leader Lord <a href="/wiki/John_Russell,_1st_Earl_Russell" title="John Russell, 1st Earl Russell">John Russell</a>, presented petitions  assembled by the main Nonconformist pressure group, the United Committee, which represented Congregationalist, Baptists, and Unitarians.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Their demand was the immediate repeal of the hated laws.  Wellington and  Peel originally were opposed, but then tried to compromise.  They finally gave, splitting the Tory party, and signaling that the once unstoppable power of the  Anglican establishment was now unexpectedly fragile and vulnerable to challenge.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy">Foreign policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Foreign policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Three men shaped British foreign policy from 1810 to 1860, with only a few interruptions, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Stewart,_Viscount_Castlereagh" title="Robert Stewart, Viscount Castlereagh">Viscount Castlereagh</a> (especially 1812\xe2\x80\x9322). <a href="/wiki/George_Canning" title="George Canning">George Canning</a> (especially 1807\xe2\x80\x931829) and <a href="/wiki/Henry_John_Temple,_3rd_Viscount_Palmerston" title="Henry John Temple, 3rd Viscount Palmerston">Viscount Palmerston</a> (especially  1830\xe2\x80\x931865). For complete list, see <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Affairs" title="Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs">Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs</a>.\n</p><p>The coalition that defeated Napoleon was financed by Britain, and held together at the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">Congress of Vienna</a> in 1814\xe2\x80\x9315. It successfully broke Napoleon\'s comeback attempt in 1815.  Castlereagh played a central role at Vienna, along with Austrian leader <a href="/wiki/Klemens_von_Metternich" title="Klemens von Metternich">Klemens von Metternich</a>. While many Europeans wanted to punish France heavily, Castlereagh insisted on a mild peace, with France to pay 700 million livre in indemnities and lose the territory seized after 1791. He realized that harsher terms would lead to a dangerous reaction in France, and now that the conservative old-fashioned Bourbons were back in power, they were no longer a threat to attempt to conquer all of Europe. Indeed, Castlereagh emphasized the need for a "balance of power", whereby no nation would be powerful enough to threaten the conquest of Europe the way Napoleon had.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Vienna ushered in a century of peace, with no great wars and few important localized ones until the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> (1853\xe2\x80\x9356).<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Prussia, Austria, and Russia, as absolute monarchies, tried to suppress liberalism wherever it might occur. Britain first took a Reactionary position at the Congress of Vienna in 1815, but relented and broke ranks with the absolute monarchies by 1820.  Britain intervened in Portugal in 1826 to defend a constitutional government there and recognising the independence of Spain\'s American colonies in 1824.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> British merchants and financiers, and later railway builders, played major roles in the economies of most Latin American nations.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Age_of_Reform">Age of Reform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Age of Reform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_achievements">Main achievements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Main achievements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In the 1825 to 1867 era, widespread public demonstrations, some of them violent, escalated to demand reform.  The ruling Tories were dead set against anything smacking of democracy or popular rule and favored severe punishment of demonstrators, as exemplified by the <a href="/wiki/Peterloo_massacre" class="mw-redirect" title="Peterloo massacre">Peterloo massacre</a> in Manchester in 1819. The Tory ranks were cracking, however, especially when Sir <a href="/wiki/Robert_Peel" title="Robert Peel">Robert Peel</a> (1788\xe2\x80\x931830) broke away on several critical issues. Nevertheless, the Whig party gets most of the credit.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>  The middle classes, often led by <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist" title="Nonconformist">nonconformist</a> Protestants, turned against the Tories and scored the greatest gains.  For example, symbolic restrictions on nonconformists called the <a href="/wiki/Test_Act" title="Test Act">Test acts</a> were <a href="/wiki/Sacramental_Test_Act_1828" title="Sacramental Test Act 1828">abolished in 1828</a>.  Much more controversial was the repeal of severe discrimination against Roman Catholics after the Irish Catholics organized, and threatened rebellion, forcing <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Relief_Act_1829" title="Roman Catholic Relief Act 1829">major concessions in 1829</a>.\n</p><p>Financial reform, led by <a href="/wiki/William_Huskisson" title="William Huskisson">William Huskisson</a> and Peel, rationalized the tariff system, and culminated in the great repeal of the tariffs on imported grain in 1846, much to the dismay of grain farmers. The 1846 repeal of the <a href="/wiki/Corn_Law" class="mw-redirect" title="Corn Law">Corn Law</a>  established free trade as the basic principle by which British merchants came to dominate the globe, and brought cheap food to British workers. A depoliticized civil service based on merit replaced patronage policies rewarding jobs for partisan efforts. Efficiency was a high priority in government, with the goal of low taxation. Overall, taxation was about 10%, the lowest in any modern nation.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Foreign policy became moralistic and hostile to the reactionary powers on the continent, teaming up with the United States to block European colonialism in the New World through the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a> of 1823. Slavery was abolished throughout the British Empire. The Royal Navy stepped up efforts to stop international trade in slaves.\n</p><p>Municipal reform was a necessity for the rapidly growing industrial cities still labouring under a hodgepodge of centuries-old laws and traditions. When Peel took over the Home Office, he abolished the espionage and cruel punishments, ended the death penalty for most crimes, and inaugurated the first system of professional police\xe2\x80\x94who in London to this day are still called "Bobbies" in his honour. The <a href="/wiki/Municipal_Corporations_Act_1835" title="Municipal Corporations Act 1835">Municipal Corporations Act 1835</a> modernized urban government, which previously had been controlled by closed bodies dominated by Tories. Over 200 old corporations were abolished and replaced with 179 elected borough councils. Elections were to be based on registered voters, city finances had to be audited in a uniform fashion, and city officials were elected by the local taxpayers.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By far the most important of the reforms was the democratization of Parliament, which began in a small but highly controversial fashion in 1832 with the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_of_1832" class="mw-redirect" title="Reform Act of 1832">Reform Act of 1832</a>. The main impact was to drastically reduce the number of very small constituencies, with only a few dozen voters under the control of a local magnate. Industrial cities gained many of the seats but were still significantly underrepresented in Parliament. The 1831\xe2\x80\x9332 battle over parliamentary reform was, according to historian <a href="/wiki/Robert_K._Webb" title="Robert K. Webb">R. K. Webb</a>, "a year probably unmatched in English history for the sweep and intensity of its excitement."<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Every few years an incremental enlargement of the electorate was made by Parliament, reaching practically all male voters by the 1880s, and all the women by 1928.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Both parties introduced paid professional organizers who supervised the mobilization of all possible support in each constituency; about 80% of the men voted.  The Tories discovered that their conservatism had an appeal to skilled workers, and also to women, hundreds of thousands of whom were organized by the <a href="/wiki/Primrose_League" title="Primrose League">Primrose League</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Woman_suffrage" class="mw-redirect" title="Woman suffrage">Woman suffrage</a> was not on the agenda. The abolition of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>, while often discussed, was never necessary because the upper house repeatedly retreated in the face of determined House of Commons action. After defeating the first two versions of the Reform Act of 1832, the Whigs got the King to agree to appoint as many new peers as was necessary to change the outcome. He promised to do so, but convinced the Lords it would be much wiser for them to approve the law.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_process">Political process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Political process">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>A weak ruler as regent (1811\xe2\x80\x9320) and king (1820\xe2\x80\x9330), <a href="/wiki/George_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George IV of the United Kingdom">George IV</a>  let his ministers take full charge of government affairs. He was a deeply unpopular playboy. When he tried to get Parliament to pass a law allowing him to divorce his wife <a href="/wiki/Caroline_of_Brunswick" title="Caroline of Brunswick">Queen Caroline</a>, public opinion strongly supported her.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> His younger brother <a href="/wiki/William_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="William IV of the United Kingdom">William IV</a> ruled (1830\xe2\x80\x9337), but was little involved in politics.\n</p><p>After four decades of rule by Pittites and Tories the first breakthrough in reform came in the removal by a Tory government of restrictions on the careers of Protestant Nonconformists in the repeal in 1828 of the laws that required Anglican church membership for many academic and government positions.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>  Much more intense was the long battle over the civil rights of Roman Catholics. <a href="/wiki/Catholic_emancipation" title="Catholic emancipation">Catholic emancipation</a> came in 1829, which removed the most substantial restrictions on Roman Catholics in Great Britain and Ireland.  Tory Prime Minister Wellington decided that the surging crisis in largely Catholic Ireland necessitated some relief for the Catholics, although he had long opposed the idea. The other main Tory leader was Sir Robert Peel, who suddenly reversed himself on the Catholic issue and was roundly denounced and permanently distrusted by the <a href="/wiki/Ultra-Tories" title="Ultra-Tories">Ultra Tory</a> faction of die-hards.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Detail_House_of_Commons.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Detail_House_of_Commons.JPG/400px-Detail_House_of_Commons.JPG" width="400" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Detail_House_of_Commons.JPG/600px-Detail_House_of_Commons.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/25/Detail_House_of_Commons.JPG 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="370" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Detail_House_of_Commons.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A painting by <a href="/wiki/George_Hayter" title="George Hayter">Sir George Hayter</a> that commemorates the passing of the Reform Act of 1832. It depicts the first session of the newly reformed House of Commons on 5 February 1833 . In the foreground, the leading statesmen from the Lords: <a href="/wiki/Charles_Grey,_2nd_Earl_Grey" title="Charles Grey, 2nd Earl Grey">Charles Grey, 2nd Earl Grey</a> (1764\xe2\x80\x931845), <a href="/wiki/William_Lamb,_2nd_Viscount_Melbourne" title="William Lamb, 2nd Viscount Melbourne">William Lamb, 2nd Viscount Melbourne</a> (1779\xe2\x80\x931848) and the Whigs on the left; and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington" title="Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington">Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington</a> (1769\xe2\x80\x931852) and the Tories on the right.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Charles_Grey,_2nd_Earl_Grey" title="Charles Grey, 2nd Earl Grey">Earl Grey</a>, prime minister 1830\xe2\x80\x931834, and his rejuvenated <a href="/wiki/Whigs_(British_political_party)" title="Whigs (British political party)">Whig Party</a> enacted a series of major reforms: the <a href="/wiki/Poor_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Poor law">poor law</a> was updated, <a href="/wiki/Child_labour" title="Child labour">child labour</a> restricted, and, most important, the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">Reform Act 1832</a> refashioned the British electoral system.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>   In 1832 Parliament abolished slavery in the Empire with the <a href="/wiki/Slavery_Abolition_Act_1833" title="Slavery Abolition Act 1833">Slavery Abolition Act 1833</a>. The government purchased all the slaves for \xc2\xa320,000,000 (the money went to rich plantation owners who mostly lived in England), and freed the slaves, most of whom were in the Caribbean sugar islands.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Whigs became champions of Parliamentary reform by making the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">Reform Act of 1832</a> their signature measure. It sharply reduced the numbers of "rotten borough" and "pocket boroughs" (where elections were controlled by powerful families), and instead redistributed seats on the basis of population. It also broadened the franchise, adding 217,000 voters to an electorate of 435,000 in England and Wales. The main effect of the act was to weaken the power of the landed gentry, and enlarge the power of the professional and business middle-class, which now for the first time had a significant voice in Parliament. However, at this point the great majority of manual workers, clerks, and farmers did not have enough property to qualify to vote. Many of them received the vote in  1867. The aristocracy continued to dominate the Church of England, the most prestigious military and naval posts, and high society, but not business, industry or finance. In terms of national governmental policy, the democratic wishes of the entire people had become decisive.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most historians emphasize the central importance of the legislation of the 1830s\xe2\x80\x931860s, although there was a dissenting minority of scholars in the 1960s and 1970s who argued against deep meanings of Whiggish progress because each of the reforms was relatively minor in itself.  Historian Richard Davis concludes that the scholarship of the 1970s represented "a  vindication of the main outlines of the old "Whig interpretation." That is, the Reform Act of 1832 was a response to mounting popular pressure. It was "the culmination of a long historical process, and an important  turning point in the emergence of a more liberal and broadly based political  system....it deserves its old designation of \'Great.\'"<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>David Thompson has stressed the revolutionary nature of the entire package of reforms:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>In all these ways \xe2\x80\x93 the organization of the new police (by Peel as Home Secretary in the 1820s), the new Poor Law, and in the new municipal councils \xe2\x80\x93 the pattern of government in England was changed fundamentally within a single decade.  In conjunction with the removal of religious disabilities, these reforms laid the structural foundation for a new kind of State in Britain: a State in which the electoral rights and civil rights of citizens were extended and given greater legal protection, but in which the ordinary citizen was subjected to a much greater degree of administrative interference, direction, and control from the centre.  The most spectacular element in this whole process \xe2\x80\x93 the Reform Bill of 1832 \xe2\x80\x93 ensured that the state should also be partially democratized at the centre.  The full significance of 1832 in the history of the country is appreciated only if it is seen as the central change in this mini-sided transformation of an agricultural nation ruled by squires, parsons, and the wealthy landowners into an industrial nation dominated by the classes produced by industrial expansion and commercial enterprise.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Chartism">Chartism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Chartism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Chartism" title="Chartism">Chartism</a> was a large-scale popular protest movement that emerged in response to the failure of the 1832 Reform Bill to give the vote  to the working class. It lacked middle class support, and it failed repeatedly. Activists denounced the "betrayal" of the working classes and the "sacrificing" of their "interests" by the "misconduct" of the government. In 1838, Chartists issued the People\'s Charter demanding manhood suffrage, equal sized election districts, voting by ballots, payment of Members of Parliament (so that poor men could serve), annual Parliaments, and abolition of property requirements. The ruling class saw the movement as dangerous.  Multiple large peaceful meetings across England demanded change but the Chartists were unable to force serious constitutional debate.  In July 1839, however, the House of Commons rejected, by 235 votes to 46, a motion to debate the Chartists\' national petition, bearing 1.3 million signatures.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Historians see Chartism as both a continuation of the 18th century fight against corruption and as a new stage in demands for democracy in an industrial society.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership">Leadership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Leadership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Prime Ministers of the period included: <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt_the_Younger" title="William Pitt the Younger">William Pitt the Younger</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Wyndham_Grenville,_1st_Baron_Grenville" class="mw-redirect" title="William Wyndham Grenville, 1st Baron Grenville">Lord Grenville</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Portland" class="mw-redirect" title="Duke of Portland">Duke of Portland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spencer_Perceval" title="Spencer Perceval">Spencer Perceval</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Jenkinson,_2nd_Earl_of_Liverpool" title="Robert Jenkinson, 2nd Earl of Liverpool">Lord Liverpool</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Canning" title="George Canning">George Canning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_John_Robinson,_1st_Viscount_Goderich" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick John Robinson, 1st Viscount Goderich">Lord Goderich</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington" title="Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington">Duke of Wellington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Grey,_2nd_Earl_Grey" title="Charles Grey, 2nd Earl Grey">Lord Grey</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Lamb,_2nd_Viscount_Melbourne" title="William Lamb, 2nd Viscount Melbourne">Lord Melbourne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_John_Temple,_3rd_Viscount_Palmerston" title="Henry John Temple, 3rd Viscount Palmerston">Lord Palmerston</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Peel" title="Robert Peel">Sir Robert Peel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The aristocracy remained dominant: there were 200 hereditary peers in the House of Lords in 1860; by 1837 they numbered 428; in 1901, there were 592. The number rose to 622 by 1910. Reform legislation in 1832, 1867, 1884 and 1918 weakened the aristocracy in terms of its control of the House of Commons. However, it ran the government: of the 10 prime ministers under Victoria, six were peers. The seventh was the son of a duke. Two (Peel and Gladstone) emerged from the business community and only one (Disraeli) was a self-made man. Of the 227 cabinet members between 1832 and 1905, 139 were sons of peers.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Prime_Minister_Wellington">Prime Minister Wellington</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Prime Minister Wellington">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Wellington" class="mw-redirect" title="Duke of Wellington">Duke of Wellington</a></div>\n<p>Wellington, the great hero who defeated Napoleon, served as the leader of the Conservative party in the House of Lords, 1828\xe2\x80\x9346. Some writers have belittled him as a befuddled reactionary, but a consensus reached in the late 20th century depicts him as a shrewd operator who hid his cleverness behind the facade of a poorly informed old soldier. Wellington worked to transform the Lords from unstinting support of the Crown to an active player in political maneuvring, with a commitment to the landed aristocracy. He used his London residence as a venue for intimate dinners and private consultations, together with extensive correspondence that kept him in close touch with party leaders in the Commons and with leading figures in the Lords. He gave public rhetorical support to <a href="/wiki/Ultra-Tory" class="mw-redirect" title="Ultra-Tory">Ultra-Tory</a> anti-reform positions, but then deftly changed positions toward the party\'s centre, especially when Peel needed support from the upper house. Wellington\'s success was based on the 44 peers elected from Scotland and Ireland, whose election he controlled.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Prime_Minister_Grey">Prime Minister Grey</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Prime Minister Grey">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Charles_Grey,_2nd_Earl_Grey" title="Charles Grey, 2nd Earl Grey">Charles Grey, 2nd Earl Grey</a></div>\n<p>Earl Grey had promoted reform of Parliament since the 1790s, always to be defeated by the Ultra-Tories.  The breakthrough came in his success in passage of the Reform Act of 1832. He sought this as the final step of reform, rather than a first step in a long process, emphasising the urgent need in 1832 to settle the intense and growing political unrest across Britain. He believed that the respectable classes deserved to have their demands for greater representation met, but he refused to extend political power to the mass of the lower middle class and working class, saying that they were not ready to be trusted with it. He wanted to preserve the basic elements of the existing constitution by removing obvious abuses, thinking that this would strengthen aristocratic leadership. He persuaded the king to promise to create enough new peers to force the bill through the House of Lords. The king made the promise while also advising the peers to stop blocking the bill. The Reform Act was Grey\'s principal achievement;  it reflects his pragmatic, moderate and conservative character, as well as his parliamentary skills of timing and persuasion.  His cabinet was a coalition of diverse interests, so in 1834 when it divided over the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Irish church</a> question he resigned.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Prime_Minister_Palmerston">Prime Minister Palmerston</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Prime Minister Palmerston">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Henry_John_Temple,_3rd_Viscount_Palmerston" title="Henry John Temple, 3rd Viscount Palmerston">Henry John Temple, 3rd Viscount Palmerston</a></div>\n<p>Palmerston played the dominant role in shaping British foreign-policy as Foreign Secretary (1830-4, 1835\xe2\x80\x9341 and 1846\xe2\x80\x9351) and as Prime Minister (1855\xe2\x80\x9358, 1859\xe2\x80\x9365).<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> He served as <a href="/wiki/Secretary_at_War" title="Secretary at War">Secretary at War</a> in Tory governments for two decades, but switched over to the Whig coalition in 1830.  The Tories despised him thereafter as a turncoat, and many of the more radical Whigs were distrustful of his basically conservative views that saw him fainthearted about or opposed to reform measures. He typically warned on the one hand against delays and on the other hand against excessive enthusiasm for reforms, preferring compromise. He was keenly sensitive to public opinion, and indeed often shapes it through his dealings with newspaper editors.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>  When he sensed that public demand had reached an unstoppable momentum, he would work for a watered-down reform.  He routinely gave the same advice to foreign governments. Diplomats across Europe took careful note of his move from the Tories to the Whigs, and suspected him of sympathy with the reform movements which were setting off upheavals in France, Belgium and elsewhere, and which frightened the reactionary governments of the major powers Russia, Austria, and Russia.  In reality he drew his foreign policy ideals from Canning. His main goals were to promote British strategic and economic interests worldwide, remain aloof from European alliances, mediate peace in Europe, and use British naval power sparingly as needed.  He worried most about France as an adversary, although he collaborated with them as in securing the independence of Belgium from the kingdom of the Netherlands.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>  He much preferred liberal and reform-oriented nations to reactionary powers.  He placed a high priority on building up British strength in India, He spoke often of pride in British nationalism, which found favour in public opinion and gave him a strong basis of support outside Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reform_leaders">Reform leaders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Reform leaders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<h5><span id="Jeremy_Bentham_.281748.E2.80.931832.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Jeremy_Bentham_(1748\xe2\x80\x931832)">Jeremy Bentham (1748\xe2\x80\x931832)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Jeremy Bentham (1748\xe2\x80\x931832)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Bentham" title="Jeremy Bentham">Jeremy Bentham</a></div>\n<p>Jeremy Bentham was an intellectual who focused on reforming English law. He was a leading promoter of <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">utilitarianism</a> as a working philosophy of action. The "greatest happiness principle", or the principle of utility, forms the cornerstone of Bentham\'s thought. By "happiness", he understood a predominance of "pleasure" over "pain".  He is best known for his inspiration of the radical forces, helping them define those reforms that were most urgently needed and how they could be implemented.  His intellectual leadership helped achieve many of the key legal, political, economic and social reforms of the 1830s and 1840s.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> He especially influenced the reform of education, prisons, poor laws, legal procedures and Parliamentary representation.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span id="John_Bright_.281811.E2.80.931889.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="John_Bright_(1811\xe2\x80\x931889)">John Bright (1811\xe2\x80\x931889)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: John Bright (1811\xe2\x80\x931889)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/John_Bright" title="John Bright">John Bright</a></div>\n<p>John Bright built on his middle-class Quaker heritage and his collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Richard_Cobden" title="Richard Cobden">Richard Cobden</a>  to promote all varieties of humanitarian and parliamentary reform. They started with a successful campaign against the Corn Laws. These were tariffs on imported food that kept up the price of grain to placate Tory landowners. The major factor in the cost of living was the price of food, and the corn laws kept the price high. Bright was a powerful speaker, which boosted him to election to parliament in 1843.  His radical program included extension of the suffrage, land reform, and reduction of taxation. He opposed factory reforms, labour unions and controls on hours For workers, women and children, arguing that government intervention in economic life was always mistaken.  He opposed wars and imperialism. His unremitting hostility to the Crimean war led to his defeat for reelection in 1857. He was soon reelected from Birmingham, leading a national campaign for parliamentary reform to enlarge the suffrage to reach the working man.   He was intensely moralistic and distrusted the integrity of his opponents. He loathed the aristocracy that continued to rule Britain. He held a few minor  cabinet positions, but his reputation rests on his organising skills and his rhetorical leadership for reform.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Historian <a href="/wiki/A._J._P._Taylor" title="A. J. P. Taylor">A. J. P. Taylor</a> has summarized Bright\'s achievements:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>John Bright was the greatest of all parliamentary orators. He had many political successes. Along with Richard Cobden, he conducted the campaign which led to the repeal of the Corn Laws. He did more than any other man to prevent the intervention of this country (Britain) on the side of the South during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, and he headed the reform agitation in 1867 which brought the industrial working class within the pale of the constitution. It was Bright who made possible the Liberal party of Gladstone, Asquith and Lloyd George, and the alliance between middle class idealism and trade unionism, which he promoted, still lives in the present-day Labour Party.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Victorian_era">Victorian era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Victorian era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_by_Bassano.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Queen_Victoria_by_Bassano.jpg/200px-Queen_Victoria_by_Bassano.jpg" width="200" height="282" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Queen_Victoria_by_Bassano.jpg/300px-Queen_Victoria_by_Bassano.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Queen_Victoria_by_Bassano.jpg/400px-Queen_Victoria_by_Bassano.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2729" data-file-height="3851" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_by_Bassano.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Queen Victoria (1837\xe2\x80\x931901)</div></div></div>\n<p>The Victorian era was the  period of <a href="/wiki/Victoria_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Victoria of the United Kingdom">Queen Victoria\'s</a> rule between 1837 and 1901 which signified the height of the British <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> and the apex of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>. Scholars debate whether the Victorian period\xe2\x80\x94as defined by a variety of sensibilities and political concerns that have come to be associated with the Victorians\xe2\x80\x94actually begins with the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">Reform Act 1832</a>. The era was preceded by the <a href="/wiki/Regency_era" title="Regency era">Regency era</a> and succeeded by the <a href="/wiki/Edwardian_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Edwardian period">Edwardian period</a>. Victoria became queen in 1837 at age 18. Her long reign saw Britain reach the zenith of its economic and political power, with the introduction of steam ships, railroads, photography, and the telegraph. Britain again remained mostly inactive in Continental politics.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy_2">Foreign policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Foreign policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)" title="International relations of the Great Powers (1814\xe2\x80\x931919)">International relations of the Great Powers
