(1814\xe2\x80\x931919)</a></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Free_trade_imperialism">Free trade imperialism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Free trade imperialism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>After the defeat of France in the <a href="/wiki/French_revolutionary_wars" class="mw-redirect" title="French revolutionary wars">Revolutionary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> (1792\xe2\x80\x931815), the UK emerged as the principal naval and imperial power of the 19th century (with London the largest city in the world from about 1830).<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy#1815\xe2\x80\x931914" title="Royal Navy">Unchallenged at sea</a>, British dominance was later described as <i><a href="/wiki/Pax_Britannica" title="Pax Britannica">Pax Britannica</a></i> ("British Peace"), a period of relative peace in Europe and the world (1815\xe2\x80\x931914).<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> By the time of <a href="/wiki/The_Great_Exhibition" title="The Great Exhibition">the Great Exhibition</a> of 1851, Britain was described as the "workshop of the world".<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Using the imperial tools of free trade and financial investment,<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> it exerted major influence on many countries outside Europe and the empire, especially in <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>. Thus Britain had both a formal Empire based on British rule as well as an <a href="/wiki/Informal_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Informal Empire">informal one</a> based on the British pound.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Russia.2C_France_and_the_Ottoman_Empire"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia,_France_and_the_Ottoman_Empire">Russia, France and the Ottoman Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Russia, France and the Ottoman Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>One nagging fear was the possible collapse of the Ottoman Empire. It was well understood that a collapse of that country would set off a scramble for its territory and possibly plunge Britain into war. To head that off Britain sought to keep the Russians from occupying Constantinople and taking over the <a href="/wiki/Bosphorus" class="mw-redirect" title="Bosphorus">Bosphorus Strait</a>, as well as from <a href="/wiki/The_Great_Game" title="The Great Game">threatening India via Afghanistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In 1853, Britain and France intervened in the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> against Russia. Despite mediocre generalship, they managed to capture the Russian port of <a href="/wiki/Sevastopol" title="Sevastopol">Sevastopol</a>, compelling <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_I_of_Russia" title="Nicholas I of Russia">Tsar Nicholas I</a> to ask for peace.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Russo-Turkish_War_(1877%E2%80%9378)" class="mw-redirect" title="Russo-Turkish War (1877\xe2\x80\x9378)">next Russo-Ottoman war</a> in 1877 led to another European intervention, although this time at the negotiating table. The <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Berlin" title="Congress of Berlin">Congress of Berlin</a> blocked Russia from imposing the harsh Treaty of San Stefano on the Ottoman Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Despite its alliance with the French in the Crimean War, Britain viewed the Second Empire of <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_III" title="Napoleon III">Napoleon III</a> with some distrust, especially as the emperor built up his navy, expanded his empire and took up a more active foreign policy.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="American_Civil_War">American Civil War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: American Civil War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>During the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> (1861\xe2\x80\x931865), British leaders favoured the Confederate States, a major source of cotton for textile mills. Prince Albert was effective in defusing a <a href="/wiki/Trent_Affair" title="Trent Affair">war scare in late 1861</a>. The British people, however, generally favoured the Union. What little cotton was available came from New York, as the blockade by the US Navy shut down 95% of Southern exports to Britain. Trade flourished with the Union and many young men crossed the Atlantic to join the <a href="/wiki/Union_Army" title="Union Army">Union Army</a>. In September 1862, President Abraham Lincoln announced the <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">Emancipation Proclamation</a> would be issued in 90 days, thus making abolition of slavery a war goal. Britain was long opposed to slavery, itself having abolished it <a href="/wiki/Slavery_Abolition_Act_1833" title="Slavery Abolition Act 1833">some three decades earlier</a>, and any possibility of its intervention on behalf of the Confederacy ended.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> British companies built and operated fast blockade runners to ship arms into the Confederacy at considerable profit. London ignored American complaints that it allowed the building of warships for the Confederacy. The warships caused a major diplomatic row that was resolved in the <a href="/wiki/Alabama_Claims" title="Alabama Claims">Alabama Claims</a> in 1872, in the Americans\' favour by payment of reparations.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Empire_expands">Empire expands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Empire expands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Starting in 1867, Britain united most of its North American colonies as the <a href="/wiki/Dominion_of_Canada" class="mw-redirect" title="Dominion of Canada">Dominion of Canada</a>, giving it self-government and responsibility for its own defence, Canada did not have an independent foreign policy until 1931. The second half of the 19th century saw a <a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">scramble for Africa</a> among the European powers. There was talk of war with France over the <a href="/wiki/Fashoda_Incident" title="Fashoda Incident">Fashoda Incident</a> of 1898. \n</p><p>The rise of the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> after 1871 posed a new challenge, for it (along with the United States), threatened to usurp Britain\'s place as the world\'s foremost industrial power. Germany acquired a number of colonies in Africa and the Pacific, but Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Otto von Bismarck</a> succeeded in achieving general peace through his balance of power strategy. When <a href="/wiki/William_II,_German_Emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="William II, German Emperor">William II</a> became emperor in 1888, he discarded Bismarck, began using bellicose language, and planned to build a navy to rival Britain\'s.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>  Britain realized its isolation policy was useless as large-scale alliances emerged.  It restored good relations with France and the United States, and ended tensions with Russia, while the confrontation with Germany became a naval race.\n</p><p>Ever since Britain had wrested control of the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Colony" title="Cape Colony">Cape Colony</a> from the Netherlands during the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, it had co-existed with Dutch settlers who had migrated further away from the Cape and created two republics of their own. The British imperial vision called for control over these new countries, and the Dutch-speaking "Boers" (or "Afrikaners") fought back in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">War in 1899\xe2\x80\x931902</a>. Outgunned by a mighty empire, the Boers waged a guerrilla war (which certain other British territories would later employ to attain independence). This gave the British troops a difficult fight, but their weight of numbers, superior equipment, and often brutal tactics, eventually brought about a British victory. The war had been costly in human rights and was widely criticised by Liberals in Britain and worldwide. However, the United States gave London its support. The Boer republics were merged with Cape Colony and Natal into the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_Africa" title="Union of South Africa">Union of South Africa</a> in 1910; this had internal self-government, but its foreign policy was controlled by London and it was an integral part of the British Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership_2">Leadership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Leadership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Prime Ministers of the period included: <a href="/wiki/William_Lamb,_2nd_Viscount_Melbourne" title="William Lamb, 2nd Viscount Melbourne">Lord Melbourne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Peel" title="Robert Peel">Sir Robert Peel</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Russell,_1st_Earl_Russell" title="John Russell, 1st Earl Russell">Lord John Russell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Smith-Stanley,_14th_Earl_of_Derby" title="Edward Smith-Stanley, 14th Earl of Derby">Lord Derby</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Hamilton-Gordon,_4th_Earl_of_Aberdeen" title="George Hamilton-Gordon, 4th Earl of Aberdeen">Lord Aberdeen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Temple,_3rd_Viscount_Palmerston" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry Temple, 3rd Viscount Palmerston">Lord Palmerston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli,_1st_Earl_of_Beaconsfield" class="mw-redirect" title="Benjamin Disraeli, 1st Earl of Beaconsfield">Benjamin Disraeli</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Ewart Gladstone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gascoyne-Cecil,_3rd_Marquess_of_Salisbury" title="Robert Gascoyne-Cecil, 3rd Marquess of Salisbury">Lord Salisbury</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Primrose,_5th_Earl_of_Rosebery" title="Archibald Primrose, 5th Earl of Rosebery">Lord Rosebery</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Queen_Victoria">Queen Victoria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Queen Victoria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a></div>\n<p>The Queen gave her name to an era of British greatness, especially in the far-flung British Empire with which she identified. She played a small role in politics, but became the iconic symbol of the nation, the empire, and proper, restrained behaviour.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Her success as ruler was due to the power of the self-images she successively portrayed of innocent young woman, devoted wife and mother, suffering and patient widow, and grandmotherly matriarch.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disraeli">Disraeli</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Disraeli">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Disraeli,_1st_Earl_of_Beaconsfield_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13619.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Benjamin_Disraeli%2C_1st_Earl_of_Beaconsfield_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13619.jpg/170px-Benjamin_Disraeli%2C_1st_Earl_of_Beaconsfield_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13619.jpg" width="170" height="237" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Benjamin_Disraeli%2C_1st_Earl_of_Beaconsfield_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13619.jpg/255px-Benjamin_Disraeli%2C_1st_Earl_of_Beaconsfield_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13619.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/86/Benjamin_Disraeli%2C_1st_Earl_of_Beaconsfield_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13619.jpg 2x" data-file-width="287" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Disraeli,_1st_Earl_of_Beaconsfield_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13619.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Disraeli</div></div></div>\n<p>Disraeli and Gladstone dominated the politics of the late 19th century, Britain\'s golden age of parliamentary government. They long were idolized, but historians in recent decades have become much more critical, especially regarding Disraeli.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli,_1st_Earl_of_Beaconsfield" class="mw-redirect" title="Benjamin Disraeli, 1st Earl of Beaconsfield">Benjamin Disraeli</a> (1804\xe2\x80\x931881), <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_Benjamin_Disraeli" class="mw-redirect" title="Premiership of Benjamin Disraeli">prime minister 1868 and 1874\xe2\x80\x9380</a>, remains an iconic hero of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_British_Conservative_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the British Conservative Party">Conservative Party</a>. He was typical of the generation of British leaders who matured in the 1830s and 1840s. He was concerned with threats to established political, social, and religious values and elites; he emphasized the need for national leadership in response to radicalism, uncertainty, and materialism.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Disraeli was especially noted for his enthusiastic support for expanding and strengthening the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, in contrast to Gladstone\'s negative attitude toward imperialism. Gladstone denounced Disraeli\'s policies of territorial aggrandizement, military pomp, and imperial symbolism (such as making the Queen Empress of India), saying it did not fit a modern commercial and Christian nation. However Gladstone himself did not turn down attractive opportunities to expand the empire in Egypt.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Disraeli drummed up support by warnings of a supposed Russian threat to India that sank deep into the Conservative mindset. His reputation as the "Tory democrat" and promoter of the welfare state fell away as historians showed that Disraeli had few proposals for social legislation in 1874\xe2\x80\x9380, and that the 1867 Reform Act did not reflect a vision of Conservatism  for the unenfranchised working man.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> However he did work to reduce class anatagonism, for as Perry notes, "When confronted with specific problems, he sought to reduce tension between town and country, landlords and farmers, capital and labour, and warring religious sects in Britain and Ireland\xe2\x80\x94in other words, to create a unifying synthesis."<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nIn the popular culture, Disraeli was a great political hero, a status that persisted for decades after his death.  </p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Disraeli-1929-lobbycard.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Disraeli-1929-lobbycard.jpg/300px-Disraeli-1929-lobbycard.jpg" width="300" height="236" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Disraeli-1929-lobbycard.jpg/450px-Disraeli-1929-lobbycard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Disraeli-1929-lobbycard.jpg/600px-Disraeli-1929-lobbycard.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1223" data-file-height="964" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Disraeli-1929-lobbycard.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lobby card 1929</div></div></div>\n<p>Historian Michael Diamond reports that for British music hall patrons in the 1880s and 1890s, "xenophobia and pride in empire" were reflected in the halls\' most popular political heroes: all were Conservatives and Disraeli stood out above all, even decades after his death, while Gladstone was used as a villain.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>  Film historian Roy Armes has argued that after 1920 historical films helped maintain the political status quo by sustaining an establishment viewpoint that emphasized the greatness of monarchy, empire, and tradition. The films created "a facsimile world where existing values were invariably validated by events in the film and where all discord could be turned into harmony by an acceptance of the status quo."<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>  Steven Fielding finds that Disraeli was an especially popular film hero: "historical dramas favoured Disraeli over Gladstone and, more substantively, promulgated an essentially deferential view of democratic leadership."  Stage and screen actor <a href="/wiki/George_Arliss" title="George Arliss">George Arliss</a> (1868\xe2\x80\x931946) was famous for his portrayals of Disraeli, winning the Oscar as best actor for 1929\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Disraeli_(1929_film)" title="Disraeli (1929 film)">Disraeli</a></i>. Fielding says Arliss "personified the kind of paternalistic, kindly, homely statesmanship that appealed to a significant proportion of the cinema audience....Even workers attending Labour party meetings deferred to leaders with an elevated social background who showed they cared.".<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gladstone">Gladstone</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Gladstone">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Ewart_Gladstone_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/William_Ewart_Gladstone_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg/170px-William_Ewart_Gladstone_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/William_Ewart_Gladstone_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg/255px-William_Ewart_Gladstone_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/William_Ewart_Gladstone_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg/340px-William_Ewart_Gladstone_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg 2x" data-file-width="342" data-file-height="457" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Ewart_Gladstone_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_13103.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gladstone</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Ewart Gladstone</a> (1809\xe2\x80\x931898) was the Liberal counterpart to Disraeli, serving as <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_William_Gladstone" class="mw-redirect" title="Premiership of William Gladstone">prime minister four times (1868\xe2\x80\x9374, 1880\xe2\x80\x9385, 1886, and 1892\xe2\x80\x9394)</a>. His financial policies, based on the notion of balanced budgets, low taxes and laissez-faire, were suited to a developing capitalist society but could not respond effectively as economic and social conditions changed. Called the "Grand Old Man" later in life, he was always a dynamic popular orator who appealed strongly to British workers and the lower middle class. The deeply religious Gladstone brought a new moral tone to politics with his evangelical sensibility. His moralism often angered his upper-class opponents (including Queen Victoria, who strongly favoured Disraeli), and his heavy-handed control split the Liberal party. His foreign policy goal was to create a European order based on cooperation rather than conflict and mutual trust instead of rivalry and suspicion; the rule of law was to supplant the reign of force and self-interest. This Gladstonian concept of a harmonious Concert of Europe was opposed to and ultimately defeated by the Germans with a <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Bismarckian</a> system of manipulated alliances and antagonisms.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Salisbury">Salisbury</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Salisbury">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Historians portray Conservative Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gascoyne-Cecil,_3rd_Marquess_of_Salisbury" title="Robert Gascoyne-Cecil, 3rd Marquess of Salisbury">Lord Salisbury</a> (1830\xe2\x80\x931903) as a talented leader who was an icon of traditional, aristocratic conservatism.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Blake,_Baron_Blake" title="Robert Blake, Baron Blake">Historian Robert Blake</a> has concluded that Salisbury was "a great foreign minister, [but] essentially negative, indeed reactionary in home affairs".<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Professor P.T. Marsh\xe2\x80\x99s estimate is more favourable; he portrays Salisbury as a leader who "held back the popular tide for twenty years."<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Professor Paul Smith argues that, "into the \'progressive\' strain of  modern Conservatism he simply will not fit."<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Professor H.C.G. Matthew points to "the narrow cynicism of Salisbury".<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> One admirer of Salisbury, Maurice Cowling agrees that Salisbury found the democracy born of the 1867 and 1884 Reform Acts as "perhaps less objectionable than he had expected\xe2\x80\x94succeeding, through his public persona, in mitigating some part of its nastiness."<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Morality">Morality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Morality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Victorian era is famous for the Victorian standards of personal morality. Historians generally agree that the middle classes held high personal moral standards (and usually followed them), but have debated whether the working classes followed suit. Moralists in the late 19th century such as <a href="/wiki/Henry_Mayhew" title="Henry Mayhew">Henry Mayhew</a> decried the slums for their supposed high levels of <a href="/wiki/Cohabitation" title="Cohabitation">cohabitation</a> without marriage and illegitimate births. However, new research using computerized matching of data files shows that the rates of cohabitation then were quite low \xe2\x80\x94 under 5% \xe2\x80\x94 for the working class and the poor.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_20th_century">Early 20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Early 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Prime Ministers from 1900 to 1923: <a href="/wiki/Marquess_of_Salisbury" title="Marquess of Salisbury">Marquess of Salisbury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Balfour" title="Arthur Balfour">Arthur Balfour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sir_Henry_Campbell-Bannerman" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Henry Campbell-Bannerman">Sir Henry Campbell-Bannerman</a>, <a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">H. H. Asquith</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bonar_Law" title="Bonar Law">Bonar Law</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Edwardian_era:_1901.E2.80.931914"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Edwardian_era:_1901\xe2\x80\x931914">Edwardian era: 1901\xe2\x80\x931914</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Edwardian era: 1901\xe2\x80\x931914">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Edwardian_era" title="Edwardian era">Edwardian era</a></div>\n<p>Queen Victoria died in 1901 and her son <a href="/wiki/Edward_VII_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward VII of the United Kingdom">Edward VII</a>  became king, inaugurating the Edwardian Era, which was characterised by great and ostentatious displays of wealth in contrast to the sombre Victorian Era. With the advent of the 20th century, things such as motion pictures, automobiles, and aeroplanes were coming into use. The new century was characterised by a feeling of great optimism. The social reforms of the last century continued into the 20th with the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> being formed in 1900. Edward died in 1910, to be succeeded by <a href="/wiki/George_V_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="George V of the United Kingdom">George V</a>, who reigned 1910\xe2\x80\x9336. Scandal-free, hard working and popular, George V was the British monarch who, with <a href="/wiki/Mary_of_Teck" title="Mary of Teck">Queen Mary</a>, established the modern pattern of exemplary conduct for British royalty, based on middle-class values and virtues. He understood the overseas Empire better than any  of his prime ministers and used his exceptional memory for figures and details, whether of uniforms, politics, or relations, to good effect in reaching out in conversation with his subjects.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The era was prosperous but political crises were escalating out of control. Dangerfield (1935) identified the "strange death of liberal England" as the multiple crises that hit simultaneously in 1910\xe2\x80\x931914 with serious social and political instability arising from the Irish crisis, labour unrest, the women\'s suffrage movements, and partisan and constitutional struggles in Parliament. At one point it even seemed  the Army might refuse orders dealing with Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> No solution appeared in sight when the unexpected outbreak of the Great War in 1914 put domestic issues on hold. McKibbin argues that the political party system of the Edwardian era was in delicate balance on the eve of the war in 1914. The Liberals were in power with a progressive alliance of Labour and, off and on, Irish Nationalists. The coalition was committed to free trade (as opposed to the high tariffs the Conservatives sought), free collective bargaining for trades unions (which Conservatives opposed), an  active social policy that was forging the welfare state, and constitutional reform to reduce the power of the House of Lords. The coalition lacked a long-term plan, because it was cobbled together from leftovers from the 1890s. The sociological basis was non-Anglicanism and non-English ethnicity rather than the emerging class conflict emphasized by the Labour Party.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_War">Great War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Great War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_World_War_I" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the United Kingdom during World War I">History of the United Kingdom during World War I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ireland_and_World_War_I" title="Ireland and World War I">Ireland and World War I</a></div>\n<p>After a rough start Britain under <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a> successfully mobilised its manpower, industry, finances, empire and diplomacy, in league with the French and Americans, to defeat the Central Powers.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> The economy grew by about 14% from 1914\xe2\x80\x9318 despite the absence of so many men in the services; by contrast the German economy shrank 27%. The Great War saw a decline in civilian consumption, with a major reallocation to munitions. The government share of GDP soared from 8% in 1913 to 38% in 1918 (compared to 50% in 1943).<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The war forced Britain to use up its financial reserves and borrow large sums from the U.S.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Britain entered the war to protect Belgium from German aggression, and quickly assumed the role of fighting the Germans on the Western Front, and dismantling the overseas German Empire. The romantic notions of warfare that everyone had expected faded as the fighting in France bogged down into <a href="/wiki/Trench_warfare" title="Trench warfare">trench warfare</a>. Along the <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Western Front (World War I)">Western Front</a> the British and French launched repeated assaults on the German trench lines in 1915\xe2\x80\x9317, which killed and wounded hundreds of thousands, but made only limited gains. By early 1916, with number of volunteers falling off, the government imposed conscription in Britain (but was not able to do so in Ireland where nationalists of all stripes militantly opposed it) in order to keep up the strength of the <a href="/wiki/British_Army_during_World_War_I" title="British Army during World War I">army</a>. Industry turned out munitions in large quantities, with many women taking factory jobs. The Asquith government proved ineffective but when <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a> replaced him in December 1916 Britain gained a powerful and successful wartime leader.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Navy continued to dominate the seas, fighting the German fleet to a draw in the only great battle, the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jutland" title="Battle of Jutland">Battle of Jutland</a> in 1916. Germany was blockaded and was increasingly short of food. It tried to fight back with submarines, despite the risk of war by the powerful neutral power the United States. The waters around Britain were declared a war zone where any ship, neutral or otherwise, was a target. After the liner <a href="/wiki/RMS_Lusitania" title="RMS Lusitania"><i>Lusitania</i></a> was sunk in May 1915, drowning over 100 American passengers, protests by the United States led Germany to abandon unrestricted submarine warfare. In spring 1917 it resumed the sinking of all merchant ships without warning. <a href="/wiki/United_States_in_World_War_I" title="United States in World War I">The United States entered the war</a> alongside the Allies in 1917, and provided the needed manpower, money and supplies to keep them going. On other fronts, the British, French, Australians, and Japanese occupied Germany\'s colonies. Britain fought the Ottoman Empire, suffering defeats in the <a href="/wiki/Gallipoli_Campaign" title="Gallipoli Campaign">Gallipoli Campaign</a> and (initially) in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, while arousing the Arabs who helped expel the Turks from Mesopotamia and Palestine. Exhaustion and <a href="/wiki/War-weariness" title="War-weariness">war-weariness</a> were growing worse in 1917, as the fighting in France continued with no end in sight. With Russia collapsing in 1917 Germany now calculated it could finally have numerical superiority on the  Western Front. The massive German <a href="/wiki/Spring_Offensive" title="Spring Offensive">Spring Offensives</a> of 1918 failed, and with arrival of a million of the <a href="/wiki/American_Expeditionary_Forces" title="American Expeditionary Forces">American Expeditionary Forces</a> at the rate of 10,000 a day by May 1918, the Germans realized they were being overwhelmed. Germany gave up, agreeing to an <a href="/wiki/Armistice_with_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Armistice with Germany">Armistice</a> on 11 November 1918. It was actually tantamount almost to a surrender with Germany handing over her fleet and heavy weapons, and her army retreating behind the river Rhine.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By 1918, there were about five million people in the army and the fledgling <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a>, newly formed from the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Naval_Air_Service" title="Royal Naval Air Service">Royal Naval Air Service</a> (RNAS) and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Flying_Corps" title="Royal Flying Corps">Royal Flying Corps</a> (RFC), was about the same size of the pre-war army.  The almost three million casualties were known as the "lost generation," and such numbers inevitably left society scarred; but even so, some people felt their sacrifice was little regarded in Britain, with poems like <a href="/wiki/Siegfried_Sassoon" title="Siegfried Sassoon">Siegfried Sassoon</a>\'s <i>Blighters</i> criticising the war as a human failure.  The literary legacy focused on mass death, mechanized slaughter, fallacious propaganda, and deep disillusionment, thereby annihilating long-standing romanticized images of the glories of war.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Postwar">Postwar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Postwar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The war had been won by Britain and its allies, but at a terrible human and financial cost, creating a sentiment that wars should never be fought again. The <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> was founded with the idea that nations could resolve their differences peacefully, but these hopes were unfounded.\n</p><p>Following the war, Britain gained the German colony of <a href="/wiki/Tanganyika" title="Tanganyika">Tanganyika</a> and part of <a href="/wiki/Togoland" title="Togoland">Togoland</a> in Africa. Britain was granted League of Nations mandates over <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a>, which was turned into a homeland for Jewish settlers, and Iraq, created from the three Ottoman provinces in Mesopotamia; the latter of which became fully independent in 1932. Egypt, which had been a British <a href="/wiki/Protectorate" title="Protectorate">protectorate</a> since 1882, became independent in 1922, although British troops remained stationed there until 1956.\n</p><p>In domestic affairs the Housing Act of 1919 led to affordable <a href="/wiki/Council_housing" class="mw-redirect" title="Council housing">council housing</a> which allowed people to move out of decrepit inner-city slums. The slums remained for several more years, with trams being electrified long before many houses. The <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1918" title="Representation of the People Act 1918">Representation of the People Act 1918</a> gave women householders the vote, but it would not be until 1928 that full equal suffrage was achieved. Labour displaced the Liberal Party for second place and achieved major success with the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1922" title="United Kingdom general election, 1922">1922 general election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland">Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaign_for_Irish_Home_Rule">Campaign for Irish Home Rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Campaign for Irish Home Rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Ireland_(1801%E2%80%931922)" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Ireland (1801\xe2\x80\x931922)">History of Ireland (1801\xe2\x80\x931922)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_(Ireland)" title="Great Famine (Ireland)">Great Famine (Ireland)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule_movement" title="Irish Home Rule movement">Irish Home Rule movement</a></div>\n<p>Part of the agreement which led to the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Union_1800" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Union 1800">1800 Act of Union</a> stipulated that the <a href="/wiki/Penal_Laws_(Ireland)" title="Penal Laws (Ireland)">Penal Laws</a> in Ireland were to be repealed and <a href="/wiki/Catholic_emancipation" title="Catholic emancipation">Catholic emancipation</a> granted. However King <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="George III of England">George III</a> blocked emancipation, arguing that to grant it would break his <a href="/wiki/Coronation_oath" class="mw-redirect" title="Coronation oath">coronation oath</a> to defend the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Church</a>. A campaign by the lawyer <a href="/wiki/Daniel_O%27Connell" title="Daniel O&#39;Connell">Daniel O\'Connell</a>, and the death of George III, led to the concession of Catholic Emancipation in 1829, allowing Roman Catholics to sit in the Parliament of the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland. But Catholic Emancipation was not O\'Connell\'s ultimate goal, which was Repeal of the Act of Union with Great Britain. On 1 January 1843 O\'Connell confidently, but wrongly, declared that Repeal would be achieved that year. When <a href="/wiki/Potato_blight" class="mw-redirect" title="Potato blight">potato blight</a> hit the island in 1846, much of the rural population, especially in Catholic districts, began to starve.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>While government funds were supplemented by private individuals and charities, and aid from the United States, it was not enough to avert a major catastrophe. Cottiers (or farm labourers) were largely wiped out during what is known in Ireland as the "<a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_(Ireland)" title="Great Famine (Ireland)">Great Hunger</a>". A significant minority elected <a href="/wiki/Unionism_in_Ireland" title="Unionism in Ireland">Unionists</a>, who championed the Union. A <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Church of Ireland</a> (Anglican) barrister <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Butt" title="Isaac Butt">Isaac Butt</a> (1813\xe2\x80\x9379), built a new moderate nationalist movement, the <a href="/wiki/Home_Rule_League" title="Home Rule League">Home Rule League</a>, in the 1870s. After Butt\'s death the Home Rule Movement, or the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Parliamentary_Party" title="Irish Parliamentary Party">Irish Parliamentary Party</a> as it had become known, was turned into a major political force under the guidance of <a href="/wiki/William_Shaw_(Irish_politician)" title="William Shaw (Irish politician)">William Shaw</a> and a radical young Protestant landowner, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Stewart_Parnell" title="Charles Stewart Parnell">Charles Stewart Parnell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Parnell\'s movement campaigned for "Home Rule", by which they meant that Ireland would govern itself as a region within the United Kingdom. Two Home Rule Bills (1886 and 1893) were introduced by Liberal Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Ewart Gladstone</a>, but neither became law, mainly due to opposition from the Conservative Party and the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>. The issue was a source of contention throughout Ireland, as a significant majority of <a href="/wiki/Unionism_in_Ireland" title="Unionism in Ireland">Unionists</a> (largely but not exclusively based in <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a>), opposed Home Rule, fearing that a <a href="/wiki/Rome_Rule" title="Rome Rule">Catholic Nationalist</a> ("Rome Rule") Parliament in Dublin would discriminate or retaliate against them, impose Roman Catholic doctrine, and impose tariffs on industry. While most of Ireland was primarily agricultural, six of the counties in Ulster were the location of heavy industry and would be affected by any tariff barriers imposed.<sup id="cite_ref-bardon_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bardon-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Irish demands ranged from the "repeal" of O\'Connell, the "federal scheme" of <a href="/wiki/William_Sharman_Crawford" title="William Sharman Crawford">William Sharman Crawford</a> (actually devolution, not federalism as such), to the <a href="/wiki/Home_Rule_League" title="Home Rule League">Home Rule League</a> of <a href="/wiki/Issac_Butt" class="mw-redirect" title="Issac Butt">Issac Butt</a>. Ireland was no closer to home rule by the mid-19th century, and rebellions in <a href="/wiki/Young_Irelander_Rebellion_of_1848" title="Young Irelander Rebellion of 1848">1848</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fenian_Rising" title="Fenian Rising">1867</a> failed.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>O\'Connell\'s campaign was hampered by the limited scope of the <a href="/wiki/Suffrage" title="Suffrage">franchise</a> in Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The wider the franchise was expanded, the better anti-Union parties were able to do in Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Running on a platform that advocated something like the <a href="/wiki/Self-rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Self-rule">self-rule</a> successfully enacted in Canada under the <a href="/wiki/British_North_America_Act,_1867" class="mw-redirect" title="British North America Act, 1867">British North America Act, 1867</a>, Home Rulers won a majority of both county and borough seats in Ireland in 1874.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>  By 1882, leadership of the <a href="/wiki/Home_Rule_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Home Rule movement">Home Rule movement</a> had passed to <a href="/wiki/Charles_Stewart_Parnell" title="Charles Stewart Parnell">Charles Stewart Parnell</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Parliamentary_Party" title="Irish Parliamentary Party">Irish Parliamentary Party</a> (IPP). A wider franchise also changed the ideological mix among non-Irish MPs, making them more receptive to Irish demands. The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1885" title="United Kingdom general election, 1885">1885 election</a> resulted in a <a href="/wiki/Hung_parliament" title="Hung parliament">hung parliament</a> in which the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Parliamentary_Party" title="Irish Parliamentary Party">Irish Parliamentary Party</a> (IPP) held the <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_power_(parliament)" title="Balance of power (parliament)">balance of power</a>. They initially supported the <a href="/wiki/Conservatives_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservatives (UK)">Conservatives</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Minority_government" title="Minority government">minority government</a>, but when news leaked that <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a> leader <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Ewart Gladstone</a> was considering Home Rule, the IPP ousted the Conservatives and brought the Liberals into office.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gladstone\'s <a href="/wiki/First_Home_Rule_Bill" class="mw-redirect" title="First Home Rule Bill">First Home Rule Bill</a> was closely modeled on the self-government given <a href="/wiki/Constitution_Act,_1867" title="Constitution Act, 1867">Canada in 1867</a>.  Irish MPs would no longer vote in Westminster but would have their own separate Dublin parliament, which would control domestic issues. Foreign policy and military affairs would remain with London.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Gladstone\'s proposals did not go as far as most Irish nationalists desired, but were still too radical for both <a href="/wiki/Irish_unionism" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish unionism">Irish unionists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unionism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Unionism in the United Kingdom">British unionists</a>: his First Home Rule Bill was defeated in the House of Commons following a split in his own party. Liberal leader <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Chamberlain" title="Joseph Chamberlain">Joseph Chamberlain</a> led the battle against Home Rule in Parliament. He broke with the Gladstone and in 1886 formed a new party, the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Unionist_Party" title="Liberal Unionist Party">Liberal Unionist Party</a>. It helped defeat Home Rule and eventually merged with the Conservative party.  Chamberlain used anti-Catholicism to built a base for the new party among "Orange" Nonconformist Protestant elements in Britain and Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>  Liberal Unionist <a href="/wiki/John_Bright" title="John Bright">John Bright</a> coined the party\'s catchy slogan, "Home rule means Rome rule."<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gladstone took the issue to the people in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1886" title="United Kingdom general election, 1886">the 1886 election</a>, but the unionists (Conservatives plus Liberal Unionists) won a majority.  In 1890 a divorce case showed Parnell was an adulterer; he was forced from power, and died in 1891.    Gladstone introduced a <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Ireland_Bill_1893" title="Government of Ireland Bill 1893">Second Home Rule Bill</a> in 1893, which this time was passed by the Commons, but was defeated  in the Conservative-dominated House of Lords.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>  The Conservatives came to power until 1906 and Home Rule was a dead issue, but the subsidized sale of farm land greatly reduced the Protestant presence in Ireland south of Ulster.  The Irish nationalists forces were rejected by the Conservatives and had no choice but to support the minority Liberal Party. New groups split off and they finally all merged in 1900 into the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Parliamentary_Party" title="Irish Parliamentary Party">Irish Parliamentary Party</a> led by <a href="/wiki/John_Redmond" title="John Redmond">John Redmond</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Conservative government also felt that the demands in Ireland could be satisfied by helping the Catholics purchase their farms from Protestant owners.  A solution by money not force was called "killing home rule with kindness".<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>  Reforms passed as a result included the <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_(Ireland)_Act_1898" title="Local Government (Ireland) Act 1898">Local Government (Ireland) Act 1898</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Land_Acts#Wyndham_Land_(Purchase)_Act_1903" title="Irish Land Acts">Wyndham Land Act</a>.  Between 1868 and 1908: spending on Ireland was generally increased, huge tracts of land were purchased from landlords and redistributed to smallholders, local government was democratised, and the franchise widely extended.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>  Ireland remained calm until the eve of the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a>,  when the Liberal government passed the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Ireland_Act_1914" title="Government of Ireland Act 1914">Third Home Rule Act</a> and Protestants in Ulster mobilized to oppose it by force.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ulster_Protestants" title="Ulster Protestants">Ulster Protestants</a> began to arm and <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Volunteers" title="Ulster Volunteers">form militias</a> ready to fight; senior leaders of the British Army indicated they would not move to suppress the Protestants (the <a href="/wiki/Curragh_Incident" class="mw-redirect" title="Curragh Incident">Curragh Incident</a>). Suddenly war with Germany broke out and home rule <a href="/wiki/Suspensory_Act_1914" title="Suspensory Act 1914">was suspended</a> for the duration. Military service was optional; there was no conscription in Ireland. Large proportions of both Protestant and Catholic young men <a href="/wiki/Kitchener%27s_Army" title="Kitchener&#39;s Army">volunteered to fight</a> Germany.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Irish_Rebellion">Irish Rebellion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Irish Rebellion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Republic_of_Ireland.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Republic_of_Ireland.svg/100px-Republic_of_Ireland.svg.png" width="100" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Republic_of_Ireland.svg/150px-Republic_of_Ireland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Republic_of_Ireland.svg/200px-Republic_of_Ireland.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="675" data-file-height="1180" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Republic_of_Ireland.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The section in red left the UK in 1922.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Easter_Rising" title="Easter Rising">Easter Rising</a> of 1916, using arms supplied by Germany was badly organized. The British army suppressed it after a week of fighting but the quick executions of 15 leaders alienated Catholic and nationalist opinion.  Overnight there was a movement away from home rule and toward Irish independence. The Cabinet decided that the 1914 Act should be brought into operation immediately  and a Government established in Dublin.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>  Negotiations were stalemated as Ulster mobilized.  London made a second attempt to implement Home Rule in 1917, with the calling of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Convention" title="Irish Convention">Irish Convention</a>. Prime Minister Lloyd George sought a <i>dual policy</i> in April 1918 that attempted to link implementing Home Rule with extending conscription to Ireland. Catholics rejected conscription and a wave of <a href="/wiki/Conscription_Crisis_of_1918" title="Conscription Crisis of 1918">anti-conscription demonstrations</a> signalled the insistent demand for total independence.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>  The old Irish Party collapsed and a militant new force united Catholics, the <a href="/wiki/Sinn_F%C3%A9in" title="Sinn F\xc3\xa9in">Sinn F\xc3\xa9in</a>, which called for <a href="/wiki/Physical_force_Irish_republicanism" title="Physical force Irish republicanism">force to achieve its goals</a>.  Sinn F\xc3\xa9in won <a href="/wiki/Irish_general_election,_1918" title="Irish general election, 1918">the 1918 general elections</a>. London\'s solution was the establishment of two Irish parliaments to pave the way for the Fourth Home Rule Bill, enacted as the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Ireland_Act_1920" title="Government of Ireland Act 1920">Government of Ireland Act 1920</a>. On 6 December 1922, Ireland formed a new dominion named the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a>. As expected, "<a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>" (six counties in Ulster), immediately exercised its right under the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish_Treaty" title="Anglo-Irish Treaty">Anglo-Irish Treaty</a> to opt out of the new state.  The union of Great Britain with most of Ulster was renamed the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland</a>, and is known by this name to the present time.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_monarchs">List of monarchs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: List of monarchs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:George_V_of_the_united_Kingdom.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/George_V_of_the_united_Kingdom.jpg/150px-George_V_of_the_united_Kingdom.jpg" width="150" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/George_V_of_the_united_Kingdom.jpg/225px-George_V_of_the_united_Kingdom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/George_V_of_the_united_Kingdom.jpg/300px-George_V_of_the_united_Kingdom.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1251" data-file-height="1759" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:George_V_of_the_united_Kingdom.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>George V, the last King to be styled as King of the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</div></div></div>\n<p>Until 1927, the monarch\'s royal title included the words "of the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland". In 1927, the words "United Kingdom" were removed from the royal title so that the monarch was instead styled as "King/Queen of Great Britain, Ireland...[and other places]". The words "United Kingdom" were restored to the monarch\'s title in 1953 with the reference to "Ireland" replaced with a reference to "Northern Ireland".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">George III</a> (1801\xe2\x80\x931820; monarch from 1760)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/George_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George IV of the United Kingdom">George IV</a> (1820\xe2\x80\x931830)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/William_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="William IV of the United Kingdom">William IV</a> (1830\xe2\x80\x931837)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Victoria,_Queen_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Victoria, Queen of the United Kingdom">Victoria</a> (1837\xe2\x80\x931901)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Edward_VII_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward VII of the United Kingdom">Edward VII</a> (1901\xe2\x80\x931910)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/George_V_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="George V of the United Kingdom">George V</a> (1910\xe2\x80\x931922; title used until 1927 but remained monarch until his death in 1936)</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a>, covers social &amp; cultural history</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Ireland_(1801%E2%80%931923)" title="History of Ireland (1801\xe2\x80\x931923)">History of Ireland (1801\xe2\x80\x931923)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="History of the United Kingdom">History of the United Kingdom</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Historiography_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Historiography of the United Kingdom">Historiography of the United Kingdom</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Terminology_of_the_British_Isles" title="Terminology of the British Isles">Terminology of the British Isles</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Politics_in_the_British_Isles" title="Politics in the British Isles">Politics in the British Isles</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Historiography_of_the_British_Empire" title="Historiography of the British Empire">Historiography of the British Empire</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Ferguson, Niall (2004). <i>Empire, The rise and demise of the British world order and the lessons for global power</i>.
